     <p>The <b>University of Florida</b> (commonly referred to as <b>Florida</b>, <b>UF</b>, or <b>U of F</b>) is an American <a href="/wiki/Public_university" title="Public university">public</a> <a href="/wiki/Land-grant_university" title="Land-grant university">land-grant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sea_grant_colleges" title="Sea grant colleges" class="mw-redirect">sea-grant</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Space_grant_colleges" title="Space grant colleges" class="mw-redirect">space-grant</a> <a href="/wiki/Research_university" title="Research university" class="mw-redirect">research university</a> located on a 2,000-acre (8.1&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>) campus in <a href="/wiki/Gainesville,_Florida" title="Gainesville, Florida">Gainesville, Florida</a>. It a senior member of the <a href="/wiki/State_University_System_of_Florida" title="State University System of Florida">State University System of Florida</a> and traces its historical origins to 1853,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> and has operated continuously on its present Gainesville campus since September 1906.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> The University of Florida is ranked 17th overall among all public national universities in the current 2013 <i><a href="/wiki/U.S._News_%26_World_Report" title="U.S. News &amp; World Report">U.S. News &amp; World Report</a></i> rankings,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> and consistently ranks within the top 100 universities worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-arwu_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arwu-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> The University of Florida was also described as a &quot;<a href="/wiki/Public_Ivy" title="Public Ivy">Public Ivy</a>&quot; in the book <i>The Public Ivies: America's Flagship Public Universities</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The University of Florida is an elected member of the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_American_Universities" title="Association of American Universities">Association of American Universities</a> (AAU), an organization composed of sixty-one American and Canadian research universities.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Due to its very high research activity, the University has been classified as an <a href="/wiki/List_of_research_universities_in_the_United_States" title="List of research universities in the United States">RU/VH Research University</a>, the highest level in the <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Classification_of_Institutions_of_Higher_Education" title="Carnegie Classification of Institutions of Higher Education">Carnegie Classification</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> The university is accredited by the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Association_of_Colleges_and_Schools" title="Southern Association of Colleges and Schools">Southern Association of Colleges and Schools</a> (SACS).</p> 
     <p>It is the second largest Florida university by <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_United_States_university_campuses_by_enrollment" title="List of largest United States university campuses by enrollment" class="mw-redirect">student population</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-sun_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sun-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> and is the seventh largest single-campus university in the United States with 49,589 students enrolled for the fall 2011 semester.<sup id="cite_ref-oipr-enrollment_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oipr-enrollment-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> The University of Florida is home to sixteen academic colleges and more than 150 research centers and institutes. It offers multiple graduate professional programs—including <a href="/wiki/Business_administration" title="Business administration">business administration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">law</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>—on one contiguous campus, and administers 123 <a href="/wiki/Master%27s_degree" title="Master's degree">master's degree</a> programs and seventy-six <a href="/wiki/Doctorate" title="Doctorate">doctoral degree</a> programs in eighty-seven schools and departments. As of fall 2012, Florida ranked fourteenth among all institutions in the number of new <a href="/wiki/National_Merit_Scholarship_Program" title="National Merit Scholarship Program">National Merit Scholars</a> enrolled.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The University of Florida's intercollegiate sports teams, commonly known by their &quot;<a href="/wiki/Florida_Gators" title="Florida Gators">Florida Gators</a>&quot; nickname, compete in <a href="/wiki/National_Collegiate_Athletic_Association" title="National Collegiate Athletic Association">National Collegiate Athletic Association</a> (NCAA) <a href="/wiki/Division_I_(NCAA)" title="Division I (NCAA)">Division I</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_Conference" title="Southeastern Conference">Southeastern Conference</a> (SEC). In their 107-year history, the university's varsity sports teams have won thirty-one national team championships, twenty-six of which are NCAA titles, and Gator athletes have won 256 individual national championships.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <table id="toc" class="toc"> 
      <tbody>
       <tr>

     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_University_of_Florida" title="History of the University of Florida">History of the University of Florida</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Dsg_UF_Century_Tower_20050507.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Dsg_UF_Century_Tower_20050507.jpg/275px-Dsg_UF_Century_Tower_20050507.jpg" width="275" height="438" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Dsg_UF_Century_Tower_20050507.jpg/413px-Dsg_UF_Century_Tower_20050507.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/41/Dsg_UF_Century_Tower_20050507.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Dsg_UF_Century_Tower_20050507.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Century_Tower_(University_of_Florida)" title="Century Tower (University of Florida)">Century Tower</a>, begun in 1953, to commemorate the 100th anniversary of the founding of university and as a tribute to the alumni who perished in both 
        <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> and 
        <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The University of Florida traces its origins to 1853, when the <a href="/wiki/East_Florida_Seminary" title="East Florida Seminary" class="mw-redirect">East Florida Seminary</a>, the oldest of the University of Florida's four predecessor institutions, was founded in <a href="/wiki/Ocala,_Florida" title="Ocala, Florida">Ocala, Florida</a>.</p> 
     <p>On January 6, 1853, <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Florida" title="Governor of Florida" class="mw-redirect">Florida Governor</a> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Brown_(Florida_politician)" title="Thomas Brown (Florida politician)">Thomas Brown</a> signed a bill that provided public support for <a href="/wiki/Higher_education" title="Higher education">higher education</a> in the state of <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> Gilbert Kingsbury was the first person to take advantage of the legislation, and established the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_University_of_Florida" title="History of the University of Florida">East Florida Seminary</a>. The East Florida Seminary was the first state-supported institution of higher learning in Florida.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> James Henry Roper, an educator from <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Florida_Senate" title="Florida Senate">state senator</a> from <a href="/wiki/Alachua_County" title="Alachua County" class="mw-redirect">Alachua County</a>, built a school, the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_University_of_Florida" title="History of the University of Florida">Gainesville Academy</a>, around the same time. In 1866, after East Florida Seminary had closed during the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> Roper offered his land and school to the State of Florida in exchange for the relocation of <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_University_of_Florida" title="History of the University of Florida">East Florida Seminary</a> to Gainesville.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The second major precursor to the University of Florida was the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Agricultural_College" title="Florida Agricultural College" class="mw-redirect">Florida Agricultural College</a>, established at <a href="/wiki/Lake_City,_Florida" title="Lake City, Florida">Lake City</a> by Jordan Probst in 1884. Florida Agricultural College became the state's first <a href="/wiki/Land-grant_college" title="Land-grant college" class="mw-redirect">land-grant college</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Morrill_Act" title="Morrill Act" class="mw-redirect">Morrill Act</a>. In 1903, the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Legislature" title="Florida Legislature">Florida Legislature</a>, desiring to expand the school's outlook and curriculum beyond its agricultural and engineering origins, changed the name of Florida Agricultural College to the &quot;University of Florida,&quot; a name that the school would hold for only two years.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id=".22University_of_the_State_of_Florida.22">&quot;University of the State of Florida&quot;</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: &quot;University of the State of Florida&quot;">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>In 1905, the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Legislature" title="Florida Legislature">Florida Legislature</a> passed the <a href="/wiki/Henry_Holland_Buckman" title="Henry Holland Buckman">Buckman Act</a>, which consolidated the existing publicly supported higher education institutions of the state. The member of the legislature who wrote the act, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Holland_Buckman" title="Henry Holland Buckman">Henry Holland Buckman</a>, later became the namesake of <a href="/wiki/Buckman_Hall" title="Buckman Hall" class="mw-redirect">Buckman Hall</a>, one of the university's oldest buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> The Buckman Act organized the <a href="/wiki/State_University_System_of_Florida" title="State University System of Florida">State University System of Florida</a> and created the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Board_of_Control" title="Florida Board of Control">Florida Board of Control</a> to govern the system. The act abolished the six pre-existing state-supported institutions of higher education, and consolidated the assets and academic programs of four of them to form the new &quot;<a href="/wiki/History_of_the_University_of_Florida" title="History of the University of Florida">University of the State of Florida</a>.&quot; The four predecessor institutions consolidated to form the new university included the <b>University of Florida at Lake City</b> (formerly Florida Agricultural College) in <a href="/wiki/Lake_City,_Florida" title="Lake City, Florida">Lake City</a>, the <b>East Florida Seminary</b> in Gainesville, the <b>St. Petersburg Normal and Industrial School</b> in <a href="/wiki/St._Petersburg,_Florida" title="St. Petersburg, Florida">St. Petersburg</a>, and the <b>South Florida Military College</b> in <a href="/wiki/Bartow,_Florida" title="Bartow, Florida">Bartow</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The Buckman Act also consolidated the colleges and schools into three institutions segregated by race and gender—the University of the State of Florida for white men, the Florida Female College for white women, and the State Normal School for Colored Students for African-American men and women.<sup id="cite_ref-State_Library_and_Archives_of_Florida_-_The_Florida_Memory_Project_Timeline_.28see_1905.29_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-State_Library_and_Archives_of_Florida_-_The_Florida_Memory_Project_Timeline_.28see_1905.29-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The City of Gainesville, led by its Mayor <a href="/wiki/William_Reuben_Thomas" title="William Reuben Thomas">William Reuben Thomas</a>, campaigned to be home to the new university.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> On July 6, 1905, the Board of Control selected <a href="/wiki/Gainesville,_Florida" title="Gainesville, Florida">Gainesville</a> for the new university campus. <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Sledd" title="Andrew Sledd">Andrew Sledd</a>, president of the pre-existing <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_University_of_Florida" title="History of the University of Florida">University of Florida at Lake City</a>, was selected to be the first president of the new University of the State of Florida. The 1905-1906 academic year was a year of transition; the new University of the State of Florida was legally created, but operated on the campus of the old University of Florida in Lake City until the first buildings on the new campus in Gainesville were completed. Architect William A. Edwards designed the first official campus buildings in the <a href="/wiki/Gothic_Revival_architecture" title="Gothic Revival architecture">Collegiate Gothic</a> style. Classes began on the new Gainesville campus on September 26, 1906, with 102 students enrolled.</p> 
     <p>In 1909, the name of the school was officially simplified from the &quot;University of the State of Florida&quot; to the &quot;University of Florida.&quot;</p> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Alligator" title="Alligator">alligator</a> was incidentally chosen as the school mascot in 1911, after a local vendor ordered and sold school pennants with an alligator emblem imprinted on them. The school colors, orange and blue, are believed to be derived from the blue and white school colors of the Florida Agricultural College in Lake City and the orange and black colors of the East Florida Seminary at Gainesville.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:MurphreeStatue.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/MurphreeStatue.jpg/220px-MurphreeStatue.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/MurphreeStatue.jpg/330px-MurphreeStatue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/MurphreeStatue.jpg/440px-MurphreeStatue.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:MurphreeStatue.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Statue of 
        <a href="/wiki/Albert_Murphree" title="Albert Murphree" class="mw-redirect">Albert Murphree</a>, the second president of the university.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="College_reorganization">College reorganization</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: College reorganization">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>In 1909, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Murphree" title="Albert Murphree" class="mw-redirect">Albert Murphree</a> was appointed the second president of the university, and organized several of the colleges of the university, increased enrollment from under 200 to over 2,000, and he was instrumental in the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Blue_Key" title="Florida Blue Key">Florida Blue Key</a> leadership society. Murphree is the only University of Florida president honored with a statue on the campus.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Historic_Layout_University_of_Florida.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Historic_Layout_University_of_Florida.jpg/220px-Historic_Layout_University_of_Florida.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Historic_Layout_University_of_Florida.jpg/330px-Historic_Layout_University_of_Florida.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Historic_Layout_University_of_Florida.jpg/440px-Historic_Layout_University_of_Florida.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Historic_Layout_University_of_Florida.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The University of Florida campus in 1916, looking southwest.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In 1924, the Florida Legislature mandated that women of a &quot;mature age&quot; (at least twenty-one years old) who had completed sixty semester hours from a &quot;reputable educational institution&quot; would be allowed to enroll during regular semesters at the University of Florida in programs that were unavailable at Florida State College for Women. Before this, only the summer semester was coeducational, to accommodate women teachers who wanted to further their education during the summer break.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lassie_Goodbread-Black" title="Lassie Goodbread-Black">Lassie Goodbread-Black</a> from Lake City became the first woman to enroll at the University of Florida, in the College of Agriculture in 1925.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/John_J._Tigert" title="John J. Tigert">John J. Tigert</a> became the third university president in 1928. Disgusted by the under-the-table payments being made by universities to athletes, Tigert established the grant-in-aid <a href="/wiki/Athletic_scholarship" title="Athletic scholarship">athletic scholarship</a> program in the early 1930s, which was the genesis of the modern athletic scholarship plan that is currently used by the <a href="/wiki/National_Collegiate_Athletic_Association" title="National Collegiate Athletic Association">National Collegiate Athletic Association</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post_World_War_II">Post World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Post World War II">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>Beginning in 1946, there was dramatically increased interest among male applicants who wanted to attend the University of Florida, mostly returning World War II veterans who could attend college under the <a href="/wiki/GI_Bill_of_Rights" title="GI Bill of Rights" class="mw-redirect">GI Bill of Rights</a> (Servicemen's Readjustment Act). Unable to immediately accommodate this increased demand, the Florida Board of Control opened the <b>Tallahassee Branch of the University of Florida</b> on the campus of Florida State College for Women in <a href="/wiki/Tallahassee" title="Tallahassee" class="mw-redirect">Tallahassee</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> By the end of the 1946–47 school year, 954 men were enrolled at the Tallahassee Branch. The following semester, the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Legislature" title="Florida Legislature">Florida Legislature</a> returned the Florida State College for Women to coeducational status and renamed it <a href="/wiki/Florida_State_University" title="Florida State University">Florida State University</a>. This sequence of events also opened up all of the colleges that comprise the University of Florida to female students. African-American students were allowed to enroll starting in 1958.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/UF_Health_Shands_Hospital" title="UF Health Shands Hospital">Shands Hospital</a> first opened in 1958 along with the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Florida_College_of_Medicine" title="University of Florida College of Medicine">University of Florida College of Medicine</a> to join the already established <a href="/wiki/University_of_Florida_College_of_Pharmacy" title="University of Florida College of Pharmacy">College of Pharmacy</a>. Rapid campus expansion began in the 1950s and continues to the present day.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The University of Florida is one of two Florida public universities, along with <a href="/wiki/Florida_State_University" title="Florida State University">Florida State University</a>, to be designated as a &quot;preeminent university&quot; by Florida senate bill 1076, enacted by the Florida legislature and signed into law by the governor in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> As a result of this legislation, the preeminent universities now receive additional funding that is intended to improve the academics and national reputation of higher education within the state of Florida.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_and_international_prominence">National and international prominence</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: National and international prominence">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>In 1985, the University of Florida was invited to become a member of the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_American_Universities" title="Association of American Universities">Association of American Universities</a> (AAU), an organization composed of now sixty-three academically prominent public and private research universities in the United States and Canada. Florida is one of only seventeen public, land-grant universities that belong to the AAU. In 2009, President <a href="/wiki/Bernie_Machen" title="Bernie Machen">Bernie Machen</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Florida_Board_of_Trustees" title="University of Florida Board of Trustees">University of Florida Board of Trustees</a> announced a major policy transition for the university. The Board of Trustees supported the reduction in the number of undergraduates and the shift of financial and other academic resources to graduate education and research in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p><i><a href="/wiki/U.S._News_%26_World_Report" title="U.S. News &amp; World Report">U.S. News &amp; World Report</a></i> has consistently ranked the University of Florida among the nation's top institutions for several of its programs in pharmacy, the sciences, engineering and law, among others, over the past decade<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> Howard and Matthew Greene of Greene's Guides listed the school as one of approximately thirty &quot;<a href="/wiki/Public_Ivies" title="Public Ivies" class="mw-redirect">Public Ivies</a>&quot;, the country's top public universities, in their 2001 book <i>The Public Ivies</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/U.S._News_%26_World_Report" title="U.S. News &amp; World Report">U.S. News &amp; World Report</a></i> currently ranks the university as the seventeenth best public university,<sup id="cite_ref-colleges.usnews.rankingsandreviews.com_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-colleges.usnews.rankingsandreviews.com-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> and 54th overall among all national universities, public and private.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Academics">Academics</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Academics">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tuition">Tuition</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Tuition">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>For the 2008-2009 academic year, annual undergraduate tuition was $3,790 for in-state students and $20,460 for out-of-state students.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> For the 2008-2009 academic year, annual graduate tuition was $8,190 for in-state students, and $23,315 for out-of-state students. For the 2008-2009 academic year, annual law school tuition was $10,800 for in-state students, and $30,100 for out-of-state students.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> For the 2008-2009 academic year, annual medical school tuition was $23,930 for in-state students, and $51,777 for out-of-state students.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> For the 2009-2010 academic year, annual undergraduate tuition was $5,044 for in-state students and $27,321 for out-of-state students. <sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2012, the University of Florida had the 6th-lowest undergraduate tuition and fees for in-state students among flagship universities.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <table style="text-align:center; float:right; margin-left:1em; margin:auto;" class="wikitable">

     <p>University of Florida students, numbering 51,413 in Fall 2008, come from more than 130 countries, and all 50 states. The ratio of women to men is 54:46, and 32 percent are graduate and professional students. Professional degree programs include architecture, dentistry, law, medicine, pharmacy and veterinary medicine. Minority populations constitute 33.5 percent of the student body, with 10.0 percent African-Americans, 15.0 percent Hispanics, 0.5 percent Native American, and 8.0 percent Asian-Americans or Pacific Islanders.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Over 12,000 students, or nearly a quarter of University of Florida students come from the <a href="/wiki/Miami" title="Miami">Miami</a>/<a href="/wiki/South_Florida" title="South Florida">South Florida</a> area, constituting the largest group of students at the university. The majority of <a href="/wiki/Hispanic" title="Hispanic">Hispanic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jewish" title="Jewish" class="mw-redirect">Jewish</a> students at the university are <a href="/wiki/Miami" title="Miami">Miamians</a>, with an estimated 6,000 Hispanic and 10,000 Jewish students at UF. <a href="/wiki/Broward_County" title="Broward County" class="mw-redirect">Broward County</a> alone produces the most UF students followed by <a href="/wiki/Miami-Dade_County" title="Miami-Dade County" class="mw-redirect">Miami-Dade County</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>During the 2008-2009 academic year the University of Florida had the 12th highest enrollment for <a href="/wiki/Student_exchange_program" title="Student exchange program">International Students</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. In total 4,731 international students enrolled at the university and this equates to about 9 percent of the total enrollment.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> This was more than any other university in Florida. Also confirmed by <a href="/wiki/Peterson%27s" title="Peterson's">Peterson's</a> the International Student populations accounts for roughly 9.0% of the entire student body.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The University of Florida is ranked second overall in the United States for the number of bachelor's degrees awarded to African-Americans, and third overall for Hispanics.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> The university ranks fifth overall in the number of doctoral degrees awarded to African-Americans, and second overall for Hispanics, and third overall in number of professional degrees awarded to African-Americans, and second overall for Hispanics.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> The university offers many graduate programs—-including engineering, business, law and medicine—-on one contiguous campus, and coordinates 123 master's degree programs and 76 doctoral degree programs in 87 schools and departments.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rankings">Rankings</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Rankings">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <table class="infobox vcard" style="width: 22em"> 
      <tbody>
       <tr>

        <td>72</td> 
       </tr> 
      </tbody>
     </table> 
     <p>In 2013, <i><a href="/wiki/U.S._News_%26_World_Report" title="U.S. News &amp; World Report">U.S. News &amp; World Report</a></i> ranked the University of Florida as the 17th-best public university in the United States,<sup id="cite_ref-colleges.usnews.rankingsandreviews.com_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-colleges.usnews.rankingsandreviews.com-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> and 54th overall among all national universities, public and private.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> In addition, <i>U.S. News &amp; World Report</i> ranked the University of Florida 9th in the country based on &quot;yield rates&quot;—the percentages of students who actually enroll after being accepted.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Many of the University of Florida's graduate programs have received top-50 rankings from <i>U.S. News &amp; World Report</i>. In 2012, <i>U.S. News</i> ranked Florida's graduate engineering program 35th nationally,<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> with its programs in <a href="/wiki/Biological_engineering" title="Biological engineering">biological engineering</a> (ranked 3rd nationally),<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Industrial_engineering" title="Industrial engineering">industrial engineering</a> (ranked 10th nationally),<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Material_engineering" title="Material engineering" class="mw-redirect">material engineering</a> (ranked 12th nationally),<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Electrical_engineering" title="Electrical engineering">electrical engineering</a> (ranked 34th nationally),<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_engineering" title="Mechanical engineering">mechanical engineering</a> (ranked 34th nationally),<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a> (ranked 31st nationally),<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> consistently ranked among the top programs in the country over the past decade. Florida's graduate programs in <a href="/wiki/Analytical_chemistry" title="Analytical chemistry">analytical chemistry</a> (ranked 8th nationally),<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistics</a> (ranked 27th nationally),<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a> (ranked 36th nationally),<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> (ranked 36th nationally),<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> were also ranked among the top programs in the country by <i>U.S. News</i>. U.S. News &amp; World Report currently ranks Florida 6th in biological/agricultural engineering.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The 2011 ARWU's rankings for the field of <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Computer_sciences" title="Computer sciences" class="mw-redirect">computer sciences</a> ranked Florida 34th in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2013, <i>U.S. News &amp; World Report</i> ranked the Hough Graduate School of Business at the University of Florida as the 36th best graduate business school in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The 2012 <i><a href="/wiki/Academic_Ranking_of_World_Universities" title="Academic Ranking of World Universities">Academic Ranking of World Universities</a></i> list assessed the University of Florida as 72nd among world universities <sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> and 44th in the United States <sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> based on overall research output and faculty awards. In 2012, <i><a href="/wiki/Washington_Monthly" title="Washington Monthly">Washington Monthly</a></i> ranked the University of Florida 21st overall nationally.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 2013, Florida Governor Rick Scott publicly announced his support for the University of Florida to ascend into the top ten among public universities, as measured by <i>U.S. News &amp; World Report</i>. He called for additional funding to decrease the student-faculty ratio at the university.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Admissions">Admissions</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Admissions">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div style="float:right;"> 
      <p><big><b>Freshmen First Time in College Statistics</b></big><sup id="cite_ref-admit_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-admit-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

     <p>As the acceptance rate at the University of Florida has trended downward, the application process has become increasingly competitive. The university has a freshmen retention rate of 94%.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> Approximately 90 percent of incoming freshmen score above the national average on standardized exams. For the Fall of 2013, admitted freshman applicants had an average grade-point average (GPA) of 4.3, a 1967 SAT score, and a 30 ACT score.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In addition, UF admitted 1,179 <a href="/wiki/International_Baccalaureate" title="International Baccalaureate">International Baccalaureate</a> students during the fall 2009 academic year. This was more than any other university in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ufl_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ufl-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ending_early_decision">Ending early decision</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Ending early decision">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>In 2007, the University of Florida joined the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia" title="University of Virginia">University of Virginia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_North_Carolina_at_Chapel_Hill" title="University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill">University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a> when they announced that they were discontinuing their <a href="/wiki/Early_decision" title="Early decision">early decision</a> admissions in an effort to help foster economic diversity in their student bodies.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> These universities assert that early decision admissions forces students to accept an offer of admission before evaluating the <a href="/wiki/Student_financial_aid" title="Student financial aid" class="mw-redirect">financial aid</a> offers from multiple universities. The university's single application deadline has been set for November 1.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Honors_program">Honors program</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Honors program">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:UF_NewHume.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/UF_NewHume.jpg/250px-UF_NewHume.jpg" width="250" height="54" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/UF_NewHume.jpg/375px-UF_NewHume.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/UF_NewHume.jpg/500px-UF_NewHume.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:UF_NewHume.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The Honors Residential College at 
        <a href="/wiki/Hume_Hall" title="Hume Hall">Hume Hall</a> provides residential and classroom facilities for students in the Honors Program.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="rellink boilerplate seealso">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/Lombardi_Scholars_Program" title="Lombardi Scholars Program">Lombardi Scholars Program</a>&nbsp;and 
      <a href="/wiki/Reitz_Scholars_Program" title="Reitz Scholars Program">Reitz Scholars Program</a>
     </div> 
     <p>The University of Florida has a nationally recognized <a href="/wiki/Honors_course" title="Honors course" class="mw-redirect">honors program</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Honors_Program_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Honors_Program-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> After gaining acceptance to the university, students must apply separately to the Honors Program and demonstrate significant academic achievement to be accepted. There are over 100 courses offered exclusively to students in this program.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>To be invited to apply to the program, freshmen must have a weighted GPA of at least 4.0 and an SAT score of 2070 out of 2400 or an ACT score of 33. In 2011, more than 1900 students applied for 700 available seats. The Honors Program also offers housing for freshman in the <a href="/wiki/Hume_Hall" title="Hume Hall">Honors Residential College at Hume Hall</a>. The Honors Program also offers special <a href="/wiki/Scholarships" title="Scholarships" class="mw-redirect">scholarships</a>, <a href="/wiki/Internships" title="Internships" class="mw-redirect">internships</a>, research, and <a href="/wiki/Study_abroad" title="Study abroad" class="mw-redirect">study abroad</a> opportunities.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Innovation_Academy_program">Innovation Academy program</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Innovation Academy program">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:UF_Criser.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/UF_Criser.jpg/250px-UF_Criser.jpg" width="250" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/UF_Criser.jpg/375px-UF_Criser.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/UF_Criser.jpg/500px-UF_Criser.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:UF_Criser.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The Criser Hall at UF houses the IA offices
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The University of Florida offers a program called the Innovation Academy,<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> a cohort of students, presenting a small college experience at a large university. The program is intended to foster and encourage ethics, leadership, entrepreneurship and innovation. Students in the program obtain a unique academic and educational experience from the Innovation Academy program (with no additional tuition costs) in conjunction with their standard University of Florida education.</p> 
     <p>Students may apply to the Innovation Academy program by indicating interest in the program on their applications. After admitting all students, admissions will then determine which students (of those who were admitted in addition to indicating interest) will be placed into the Innovation Academy program. Students in this program complete their standard degree requirements on a spring/summer schedule instead of the traditional fall/spring schedule; all other university, college, and major requirements remain the same. Students accepted into the program essentially allow for their fall/spring admissions spot to be taken by an extra admit. This fulfills one of two main ambitions of the program, which is to make more space and access to the university by admitting more students yet streamlining resources so that no student has hindered or limited education.</p> 
     <p>Because class room and housing spaces are limited and there is a large amount of students in the Fall, Innovation Academy students attend Spring and Summer semesters while having two Fall restrictions: They cannot take physical Fall classes or have physical Fall housing. The university specifically has these restrictions for IA students because these students have opportunities (such as an exclusive minor) no other students can ever get, free of charge. In short, the small restrictions are made up for by enhancing the standard UF academics with unique opportunities (while if the university were to opt in choosing a random group of students then restricting them, that would cause problems because those students will have nothing making up for the restrictions).</p> 
     <p>Innovation Academy students, as long as they are full-time students in the Spring semester prior, do not have to be full-time students (nor do they have to be enrolled at all) in the Fall in order to pick up student tickets and attend football games. In addition, Innovation Academy students can still participate in all clubs, activities, extracurricular, and etc. Furthermore, these students have the opportunity to do internships, study abroad, research, work, and more in the Fall without having their schedules or academic career hindered. They can also take online classes at the university during the Fall and use any resources they need. The Fall restrictions may serve as limitations on social college life, however, such social limitations can be worked around and the university makes up for this by (as previously stated) enhancing the academic experience with the second ambition of the program.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:UF-BeatyTowers.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/UF-BeatyTowers.JPG/250px-UF-BeatyTowers.JPG" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/UF-BeatyTowers.JPG/375px-UF-BeatyTowers.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/UF-BeatyTowers.JPG/500px-UF-BeatyTowers.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:UF-BeatyTowers.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The Beaty Towers at UF house the IA Living Learning Community
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The third, yet primary, ambition of the Innovation Academy is to foster innovation, entrepreneurship, and creativity to students of the program via an optional Living Learning Community during freshman year, a unique and exclusive minor, service learning opportunities, guest lectures, and more. All of this, in conjunction with the academy, is meant to give a specific set of skills to enhance the standard UF academic experience, prepare students for the future economy and business, encourage innovation, creativity, and entrepreneurship as well as elevated thinking, and to present opportunities (leadership, networking, and co-op opportunities) that no other students obtain. Students in the program will be offered exclusive clubs and organizations they may join in conjunction with all other clubs and organizations offered to them.</p> 
     <p>Students interested in the program are advised to have the traits/desire for and of expert and peer collaboration, multidisciplinary learning, trial and error experimentation, strategic risk taking, and a Play, Passion, Purpose attitude. Students interested are also advised to be prospective and are also allowed to apply to the <a href="/wiki/Honors_course" title="Honors course" class="mw-redirect">Honors program</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Honors_Program_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Honors_Program-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> at UF while being in IA (which is highly advisable given that both programs each have a wealth of extra opportunities).</p> 
     <p>The long term enrollment goal is 2,000 students and the Innovation Academy program is also meant to aid the University of Florida in transitioning to a fully active, twelve-month calendar. It is part of the university's innovation project, in which the university also established the Innovation Hub and Innovation Square in Gainesville.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sustainability">Sustainability</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Sustainability">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:UF_RinkerHall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/UF_RinkerHall.jpg/220px-UF_RinkerHall.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/UF_RinkerHall.jpg/330px-UF_RinkerHall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/UF_RinkerHall.jpg/440px-UF_RinkerHall.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:UF_RinkerHall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Opened in 2003, 
        <a href="/wiki/Rinker_School_of_Building_Construction" title="Rinker School of Building Construction">Rinker Hall</a> was the first building on campus to receive 
        <a href="/wiki/Leadership_in_Energy_and_Environmental_Design" title="Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design">LEED</a> recognition. Since opening, other new and renovated buildings on campus have also received certification.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In 2005, the University of Florida became a <a href="/wiki/National_Audubon_Society" title="National Audubon Society">Certified Audubon Cooperative Sanctuary</a> for environmental and wildlife management, resource conservation, environmental education, waste management, and outreach.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Through long-term environmental initiatives, the University of Florida created an Office of Sustainability in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> Their mission is to continue to improve environmental sustainability in many different areas on campus. They have stated that their future goals are to produce zero waste by 2015, and to achieve Carbon Neutrality by 2025.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> Recently the university appointed Anna Prizzia as the University’s new Sustainability Director. UF received a &quot;B+&quot; grade on the 2009 College Sustainability Report Card <sup id="cite_ref-2009_College_Sustainability_Report_Card_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2009_College_Sustainability_Report_Card-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> for its environmental and sustainability initiatives. In 2009 &quot;B+&quot; was the second highest grade awarded by the Sustainable Endowments Institute.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colleges_and_academic_divisions">Colleges and academic divisions</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Colleges and academic divisions">edit</a>]</span></h3>
